♠ Linguistic family ♣ Turkic ♥
♠ Religion Genus ♣ Islam ♥
♠ Religion Family ♣ Sunni ♥ "Before the 16th century, the Ottomans were relatively uninterested in the differences between Shii and Sunni Islam. The Sufi orders (see Sufism), which put Ali and the prophet’s descendants at the center of their devotions, were much more popular in the early Ottoman Empire. But in 1501 Shah Ismail I (r. 1501-24) made Imami Shiism the religion of his court in Iran and began to persecute Sunni Muslims in his realm. In addition, the Turkoman tribes of eastern Anatolia rose in rebellion against the Ottomans, proclaiming Shah Ismail to be the long-awaited imam who would restore justice to the world. These rebels were known as the Kizilbaş in Ottoman texts and were ruled as heretics by the Ottoman Sunni legal establishment, making it legally permissible for the Ottoman army to kill them. The Shii rebellion against the Ottomans was temporarily ended by the Battle of Çaldiran in 1512. But the continued presence of a hostile Shii Iran played a significant role in bolstering the Ottoman attachment to Sunni Islam. With the hostile “heretic” Shia on their eastern borders and the ongoing anti-Muslim challenges from the Christian world, the Ottoman sultans were increasingly motivated to present themselves as the upholders and defenders of what they regarded as the true faith of Sunni Islam. [...] The embrace of Sunni orthodoxy by the Ottoman political and legal elite did not translate, however, into the persecution of those Shiis in the empire who accepted Ottoman rule peacefully, even if they held the view that such rule was illicit in the absence of the imam. This tolerance was especially manifested in the Ottoman treatment of the Shia of Iraq. When Süleyman I (r. 1520-66) conquered Baghdad in 1535, for instance, he endowed Shii shrines as well as Sunni ones and hosted Shii clergy along with their Sunni counterparts. Subsequent Ottoman governors extended this strategy of tolerance and even provided patronage to Shii shrines and clergy. In part, this was due to a realization that it was best not to alienate the Shii subjects of the sultan in Iraq. But it also seems to have arisen out of a more general cultural practice of tolerance that the sultans extended to both nonSunnis and non-Muslims."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_5_TrOttm3]
♠ Religion ♣ Hanafi ♥ "The Ottoman dynasty established the Hanafi tradition as the official legal interpretation of the empire, but it did not compel scholars to abandon their own legal traditions to conform to that favored in Istanbul. Nevertheless, if a scholar wished to ingratiate himself with the new regime a switch might seem prudent."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_6_TrOttm3]
♠ Alternate Religion Genus ♣ Islam ♥
♠ Alternate Religion Family ♣ Sufi ♥ "Despite sultanic support of some Sufi orders, Sufis also sometimes challenged the sultans’ authority by claiming they had access to a truth that could never be known by those who had not embarked on the path of mysticism. In the aftermath of the defeat of Sultan Bayezid I (r. 1389-1402) at Ankara in 1402, the Sufi Sheikh Bedrettin preached in favor of a social movement that sought to tear down the differences between classes and religions. His movement gained great popularity among the ordinary people of all religions and his disciple, Börlüce Mustafa, started a revolt against the Ottoman in 1416. The rebels had some initial success but the Ottoman army was finally able to defeat them and Bedrettin and thousands of his followers were executed. The Ottomans also faced other revolts led by radical Sufis in the 15th and 16th centuries. Indeed, one of the greatest challenges to the Ottoman throne came from Shah Ismail, who headed an extremist Sufi order that believed him to be the long-awaited hidden imam."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_7_TrOttm3]
♠ Alternate Religion ♣ ♥ "Once they had established their empire, the Ottoman sultans had close relations to two Sufi groups, the Mevlevi Order and the Bektaşi Order, although these two could not be more different in their religious outlook. The Mevlevi Order, founded by the son of the 13th century poet Rumi, was the order of Ottoman intellectuals and attracted poets, musicians, and calligraphers. The order established convents or hostels (tekke) in various Ottoman cities and these served as centers for the teaching of art and music as well as being places of spiritual retreat. The Bektaşi Order, by contrast, was the order of the Janissaries and its traditions were oral rather than literary. Although many of its practices were unorthodox, such as the drinking of wine, it too received official recognition from the sultans as a way of keeping the troops happy. The sultans and their families supported both orders financially by establishing some of their convents and bestowing lavish gifts on the orders’ sheikhs. [...] In the second half of the 18th century, a more austere and rigorous Sufi order, the Naqshbandiyya Order, answered some of the objections raised by the Muslim fundamentalists to Sufism. The Naqshbandis believe in a strict adherence to sharia and reject the notion of sainthood found in many other Sufi orders, but they still hold that the understanding of God that can only be gained from a mystical experience is a vital, necessary part of Islam. The sober interpretation of mysticism offered by the Naqshbandiyya Order was popular among intellectuals within the Ottoman Empire in the 18th and 19th centuries as it offered a compromise between mysticism and the interpretations of Islam that fundamentalists such as the Wahhabis were preaching. However, critics of Sufism still did not accept the Naqshbandiyya as being rooted in a proper understanding of Islam."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_8_TrOttm3]
♠ Alternate Religion Genus#2 ♣ Islam ♥
♠ Alternate Religion Family#2 ♣ Shia ♥ "Although Sunni Islam, as interpreted by the Hanafi School of law, was the state religion of the Ottoman Empire, there were sizeable Shii minorities in several of the Ottoman Arab provinces, most notably in what are today the countries of Iraq, Lebanon, and Yemen. In the first two, the Shia followed the Imami tradition. The dominant form of Islam in Iran today, the Imami tradition is known to some Western scholars as “Twelver Shiism” as its believers hold that the 12th imam, who vanished in the ninth century c.e., will return at some future time to institute a reign of absolute justice on earth. In Lebanon, the adherents of this community were known as the Mitwallis in the Ottoman period, although it is not a name they use today to refer to themselves. Both the Imami tradition and the Zaydi tradition followed by the Shia of Yemen trace the descent of their imams from the line of the sixth imam, Jaafar al-Sadiq (702-65), but the Imami trace their tradition through Sadiq’s son Musa al-Kazim while the Zaydi trace their tradition through his son Muhammad. In addition, the Alawis of Syria and the Alevis of eastern Anatolia also claim to be Shia although the Shii clergy in Iran did not accept the Alawi claim until the 20th century."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_9_TrOttm3]
♠ Alternate Religion#2 ♣ Twelver ♥ "Although Sunni Islam, as interpreted by the Hanafi School of law, was the state religion of the Ottoman Empire, there were sizeable Shii minorities in several of the Ottoman Arab provinces, most notably in what are today the countries of Iraq, Lebanon, and Yemen. In the first two, the Shia followed the Imami tradition. The dominant form of Islam in Iran today, the Imami tradition is known to some Western scholars as “Twelver Shiism” as its believers hold that the 12th imam, who vanished in the ninth century c.e., will return at some future time to institute a reign of absolute justice on earth. In Lebanon, the adherents of this community were known as the Mitwallis in the Ottoman period, although it is not a name they use today to refer to themselves. Both the Imami tradition and the Zaydi tradition followed by the Shia of Yemen trace the descent of their imams from the line of the sixth imam, Jaafar al-Sadiq (702-65), but the Imami trace their tradition through Sadiq’s son Musa al-Kazim while the Zaydi trace their tradition through his son Muhammad. In addition, the Alawis of Syria and the Alevis of eastern Anatolia also claim to be Shia although the Shii clergy in Iran did not accept the Alawi claim until the 20th century."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_10_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In the 15th century CE, the Turkic Ottoman Sultan Mehmet II conquered Constantinople, took from the last vestiges of the defeated Roman Empire the famous title 'caesar', and added to it the grandiose title 'ruler of the two continents and the two seas'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_11_TrOttm3] However, it was Suleiman I (1520-1566 CE) who earned his sobriquets 'the Magnificent' and 'the Lawgiver' when he reformed the Ottoman system of government, codified Ottoman secular law, and extended the Ottoman Empire into Europe as far as Vienna.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Population and political organization[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Ottoman Empire was a hereditary dynasty under the rule of an Ottoman Sultan.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_12_TrOttm3] The Ottoman 'slave-elite' differed from that of the Mamluk Sultanate in that the Ottoman slaves could never achieve the position of sultan, which remained the hereditary property of the Osman dynasty. With its capital in Istanbul, the main organ of state power was the 'elaborate court, palace, and household government'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_13_TrOttm3] Policy-making was weakly institutionalized: in theory, all decisions were made by the sultan himself, and so Ottoman policies were shaped by the sultan's personal character and by the 'individuals or factions who had his ear'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_14_TrOttm3] The sultans appointed their own staff and paid them with a wage or (increasingly after 1600 CE) a fief.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_15_TrOttm3] State funding came in large part from money raised by fief holders until Grand Vizier Ibrahim Pasha Kulliyesi (in office from 1718 CE) introduced a property tax.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_16_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The administrative and military officials around the sultan were slaves educated in palace schools.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_17_TrOttm3] The source of this non-Turkish administrative class was the devsirme tribute, which began in 1438 CE; by the 16th century about 1,000 boys were taken per year per recruiting province in the Balkans and non-Muslim communities in Anatolia. The system divided these slaves into those who would serve the bureaucracy and those who would form the elite military corps known as janissaries. In 1582 CE, recruits of non-devsirme origin, including free Muslims, were permitted to join the janissaries and after 1648 CE the devsirme system was no longer used to recruit for the janissaries.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_18_TrOttm3] The imperial household together with its armies and administrative officials was truly vast, numbering about 100,000 people by the 17th century.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_19_TrOttm3] The renowned Ottoman architect Sinan was a tribute slave; he notably designed the Sehzade and Süleyman külliyes (complexes of buildings including mosques and mausoleums) and the Selim Mosque at Edirne (1569-1575 CE), with its four 83-metre-high minarets.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_20_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_REF_21_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Ottoman sultans issued decrees through an imperial council (divan)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_22_TrOttm3] and the chief executive power below the sultan, the grand vizier.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_23_TrOttm3] Although certain regions (Egypt, for example) may have differed slightly in their governing structure, Ottoman regional government typically involved governors (beylerbeyi)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_24_TrOttm3] whose provinces were split into districts (sanjaks) under district governors (sanjak beyi).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_25_TrOttm3] The sanjak beyi also was a military commander.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_26_TrOttm3] Fief-holding soldiers were responsible for local law and order within their districts.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_27_TrOttm3] By the late 16th century, the lowest level of this system had transformed into a system of tax farms or fiefs given to non-military administrators.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_28_TrOttm3] In 1695 CE, these tax farms were 'sold as life tenures (malikane)', and later shares in tax farms were sold to the public.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_29_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Ottoman law was divided into religious - Islamic sharia - and secular kanun law.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_30_TrOttm3] Kanun law essentially served to fill the gaps left by the religious legal tradition, regulating 'areas where the provisions of the sacred law were either missing or too much at at odds with reality to be applicable'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_31_TrOttm3] In the Ottoman Empire, this included aspects of criminal law, land tenure and taxation; kanun law drew its legitimacy from precedent and custom.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_32_TrOttm3] Military judges (kadi'asker) were the heads of the empire's judiciary and heard cases brought before the imperial council.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_33_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Ottoman Anatolia further enhanced many aspects of Byzantine culture. In 1331, in an attempt to spread Islam to new territories, Iranian and Egyptian scholars were brought to Iznik in northwestern Anatolia to teach at the first Ottoman college.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_34_TrOttm3] Palace schools were created to train the next generation of Ottoman officials. During the 15th and 16th centuries CE, about 500 libraries were built by sultans and high Ottoman dignitaries. These were maintained by waqf religious foundations; the majority in Istanbul, Bursa and Erdine. Initially, these were madrassa libraries and specialist libraries, but the first independent Ottoman waqf libraries were founded by the Koprulu family in 1678 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_35_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Ottoman postal system (ulak) structured around postal stations (similar to the Mongol yam)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_36_TrOttm3] spanned an empire of 5.2 million square kilometres at its greatest extent,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_37_TrOttm3] with a population of approximately 28 million people in 1600 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_38_TrOttm3] Istanbul likely had a population of at least 650,000 in 1600 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_39_TrOttm3]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ [9-10] ♥ levels.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
1. Sultan[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Mehmet II also took the title "caesar" and "ruler of the two continents and the two seas"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_53_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] The Ottoman Empire was a dynastic state. Rule was passed on to male heir.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_54_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Sultans "ruled the Empire through members of their own household, whom they had appointed to government office. This was a tendency which began probaby in the late fourteenth century, and had become very pronounced by the late fifteenth."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_55_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The sultans ruled the Empire through their court as much as through formal organs of government" and sometimes by-passed formal structures of government such as in diplomatic negotiations. "There never, it seems, was a formal mechanism for policy making. All decisions in theory were the sultan’s own. What mattered, therefore, was the character of the sultan, and the individuals or factions who had his ear."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_56_TrOttm3]
"At the center of the centralizing Ottoman state was an elaborate court, palace, and household government." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_57_TrOttm3]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]

They were not hereditary positions and not held for life.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_76_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Judgeship of a town or city judge

Role of sanjak included law and order (with fief holder), pursuing bandits, investigating heresy, supplying army, materials for shipbuilding, and those on the frontier special military duties.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_80_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. Fief-holding soldiers responsible for local law and order[MAJIDBENAM_REF_81_TrOttm3] -- system declined late 16th century, reassigned as tax farms or to non-military nominees of Palace[MAJIDBENAM_REF_82_TrOttm3]
"The troops of each sanjak, under the command of their governor, would then assemble as an army and fight under the banner of the governor-general of the province. In this way, the structure of command on the battlefield resembled the hierarchy of provincial government."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_83_TrOttm3] [MAJIDBENAM_REF_84_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Fiefs were only one form of land-holder in Sanjacks. Other land was privately owned, formed part of a trust, or controlled by the Sultan. Beglik or miri land was given out by Sultan as fiefs.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_85_TrOttm3]

There were in principle twent-four beys, just as there had been twenty-four first-class emirs. The title kashif for the governors of the provinces was also an inheritance from the Mamluk sultanate."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_94_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] In Cairo there were "no specialized municipal administration or public institutions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_95_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 8? timar holders [MAJIDBENAM_REF_96_TrOttm3]

3. Vassal provinces
"In matters of provincial government, the empire was never truly centralized. In the fourteenth and fifteenth centuries, it was still common for newly conquered regions to remain vassal provinces, under the control of their former lords, often Christians, in return for tribute and military manpower."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_120_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The experience of Ragusa (modern Dubrovnik is a good illustration of the accommodating method operating on both sides. Its citizens had petitioned the Pope for permission to trade with infidels right after the Turks' first serious victory in Europe. By the 15th century the Ottomans had turned Ragusa into their own Venice, to every successive doge's fury and despair! The Ragusans' behaviour was so mild and noble that by 1347 they had erected an old people's home. By the mid-15th century they had abolished slave trading, forbidden torture, organised a dole, a public health service, a town planning institute and several schools."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_121_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Less compliant Ottoman vassal rulers were subjected to a number of requirements. They were forced to send their sons to the Ottoman court as hostages, had to pay tribute and to take part in the Ottoman wars either in person or represented by their sons. Control over their compliance was exercised by the watchful beys of the marches."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_122_TrOttm3]
♠ Religious levels ♣ 4 ♥[MAJIDBENAM_REF_123_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. Sultan[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Suleiman I called himself "caliph of all the Muslims in the world" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_124_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Chief Mufti
called seyhulislam. Part of the ulema religious establishment.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Inner Circle
called ilmiye. Part of the ulema religious establishment.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. Imams
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The population of the Empire was heterogenous in religion, language and social structure. As the Faith of the sultans and of the ruling elite, Islam was the dominant religion, but the Greek and Armenian Orthodox Churches retained an important place within the political structure of the Empire, and ministered to large Christian populations which, in many areas, outnumbered Muslims." There were also Jews (especially after expelled from Spain 1492), Maronites and Druzes.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_125_TrOttm3]

These led them to theş local sancak bey's two-horse-tail standard. The men of each sancak then assembled around a provincial governor or beylerbeyi before riding to the Sultan's camp."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_126_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Janissaries were organized into ortas (regiments) of 100 - 3,000 men.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_127_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥ Copyists, calligraphers, booksellers. Donkey drivers, porters, water carriers, peddlers, day laborers. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_131_TrOttm3] Grain milling, vinegarmaking. Spinning, weaving, dyeing, leatherworking, woodworking, metalworking. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_132_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Professions[MAJIDBENAM_REF_133_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Coptic professions in Egypt
Goldsmiths, jewelers, shoemakers, masons, engravers, carpenters, weavers, tailors, furriers, construction workers, laborers, candlemakers, puveyors of wines and distilled alcoholic drinks (especilly araq), bureaucrats, financial and tax administrators, secretaries, stewards to the political class, priest.
♠ Professional military officers ♣ present ♥Emir Orhan: "A regularly paid force of Muslim and Christian cavalry and infantry was created by his vizier, Allah al Din. The horsemen were known as müsellems (tax-free men) and were organised under the overall command of sancak beys into hundreds, under subaşis, and thousands, under binbaşis. The foot-soldiers, or yaya, were comparably divided into tens, hundreds and thousands. These infantry archers occasionally fought for Byzantium, where they were known as mourtatoi. Müsellems and yayas were at first paid wages, but by the time of Murat I (1359) they were normally given lands or fiefs in return for military service, the yayas also having special responsibility for the protection of roads and bridges." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_134_TrOttm3] "Both [yaya] and the müsellems were gradually relegated to second-line duties late in the 14th century, and by 1600 such units had either been abolished or reduced to non-military functions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_135_TrOttm3]
♠ Professional soldiers ♣ present ♥ Salaried personnel increased from about 24,000 in 1527 CE to just under 100,000 in 1660 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_136_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"By 1400, therefore, most of the troops in the Ottoman army served on a contractual basis, allowing the sultan to levy a predictable number of reliable troops year after year."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_137_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Emir Orhan: "A regularly paid force of Muslim and Christian cavalry and infantry was created by his vizier, Allah al Din. The horsemen were known as müsellems (tax-free men) and were organised under the overall command of sancak beys into hundreds, under subaşis, and thousands, under binbaşis. The foot-soldiers, or yaya, were comparably divided into tens, hundreds and thousands. These infantry archers occasionally fought for Byzantium, where they were known as mourtatoi. Müsellems and yayas were at first paid wages, but by the time of Murat I (1359) they were normally given lands or fiefs in return for military service, the yayas also having special responsibility for the protection of roads and bridges." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_138_TrOttm3] "Both [yaya] and the müsellems were gradually relegated to second-line duties late in the 14th century, and by 1600 such units had either been abolished or reduced to non-military functions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_139_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Janissaries were paid a monthly salary.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_140_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In Egypt, "janissaries and the azab drew a large part of their income ... from urban tax-farms (muqata'at) and from the payment they received for the protection of artisans and shopkeepers in the city."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_141_TrOttm3]
♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ "Religious employees included the imams , the hatibs and the muezzin, who led daily prayers and served in local mosques. Some state employees, such as the muftis, the kadıs and the muderris, had both a legal and religious identity. The Ulema, scholars of the Quran and the holy law, are not priests in the sense of rituals etc."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_142_TrOttm3]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_143_TrOttm3] "By the seventeenth century, the imperial household, including the armies and the administration, numbered about 100,000 people." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_144_TrOttm3]
♠ Source of support ♣ land; governed population; state salary ♥"By the sixteenth century, the sultan governed his domains largely through the 'Slaves of the Porte'. These were the men whom he had recruited to serve as ministers, provincial governors or troops, and whom he paid through the Treasury or by the allocation of fiefs."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_145_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Most provinces under salaried officials by 1550 CE. They collected taxes and sent excess to central government.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_146_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Payment to the clerks could be a wage or come in the form of a fief, with fief holders clearly predominating by 1600 or later."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_147_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Funding relied on money raised by fief holders until Grand Vizier Ibrahim Pasha Kulliyesi introduced a property tax c1718 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_148_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries the Ottoman state was "a centralized patrimonial state based on slave elites and timar (quasi-feudal grants of tax revenue equivalent to iqta's) holding soldiers, and on an imperial ideology." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_149_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Sultan assigned revenues to military and administrative servants and a portion to charity. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_150_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In Egypt, "Mamluk emirs derived their power from exploiting the peasantry (fallahin) through rural tax-farms, but most of the lived in Cairo." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_151_TrOttm3]
♠ Examination system ♣ present ♥ "Appointments to judgeships required the attainment of appropriate levels in the educational system." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_152_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The iç oğlani were trained forup to seven years in palace schools which concentrated on character-building, leadership, military and athletic prowess, languages, religion, science, and a creative art of the pupil's choosing. Three further examinations selected men for the Kapikulu cavalry, to be Kapikulu officers and, at the top of the tree, to become military or administrative leaders. All remained bachelors until their training ended, when most married women who had been through a parallel schooling in the Palace harem."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_153_TrOttm3]
♠ Merit promotion ♣ present ♥Bureaucracy was staffed mostly from a slave class of boys raised from the devsirme tribute system, every five years, from Christian families (mostly from the Balkans region). They were taught Turkish, converted to Islam and educated from childhood to work in the military and government, excluding sons of most Muslim fathers within the Empire. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_154_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Appointments to judgeships required the attainment of appropriate levels in the educational system." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_155_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Present in the Egyptian financial administration: "All the positions of Efendi in the Treasury were established as Muqata'at which were distributed when vacant at auctions held in the house of the Ruznameji to the highest bidder from amongst those members qualified to hold them, and whose price was delivered to the Vali as part of his Hulvan revenues. For that reason, the departments were also called Muqata'a and the Efendis Muqata'a'i, "holder of the Muqata'a",in the registers. Only those possessing the requisite qualifications, as manifested by prior membership in the scribal corporation of an Imperial Treasury, whether in Egypt or elsewhere in the Ottoman Empire, could bid for these positions." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_156_TrOttm3]
♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ present ♥ Customs house at Suez. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_157_TrOttm3]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥ From 15th century Ottomans had secular law called kanun which coexisted with the religious law, shari'a.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_158_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Kanun regulated areas where the provisions of the sacred law were either missing or too much at at odds with reality to be applicable. These, in the Ottoman Empire as in other Islamic polities, were above all in the areas of criminal law, land tenure, and taxation. The origins of the secular law lay in custom, and it was long usage that in the first place gave it legitimacy." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_159_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Justice system was the seriat, Islamic law, decided by the ulema religious establishment. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_160_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Mehmet II "promulgated the first systematic legal codes dealing with the organization of the state and the obligations of subjects." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_161_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"In matters of government administration, Ottomman law applied to all subjects, but in matters of family and business law, it applied only to Muslims. Non-Muslims had their own communal law and courts. In practice, however, Jews and Christians commonly had recourse to Ottoman courts in order to assure enforcement, or to have state guarantees for commercial and property transactions, or to win an advantage in marital and inheritance disputes." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_162_TrOttm3]
♠ Judges ♣ present ♥ Called a Kadi. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_163_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The Ottoman state appointed all important judges, jurisconsults, and professors of law." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_164_TrOttm3] Military judges (kadi'asker) were the "chief judges of the Empire, who were responsible for judicial matters that came before the council."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_165_TrOttm3] Judges in towns.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_166_TrOttm3]
♠ Courts ♣ present ♥ State courts. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_167_TrOttm3] Ottoman Cairo had fifteen courts of justice. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_168_TrOttm3]
♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ present ♥ "The Ottoman state appointed all important judges, jurisconsults, and professors of law." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_169_TrOttm3]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥
♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥
♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ 1399 CE hospital in Bursa founded by Bayezid I. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_170_TrOttm3] Hospices for travellers and students in towns and villages.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_171_TrOttm3] Reconstruction of Istanbul: schools, hospitals, baths, caravansaries, bazaars, bakeries, mills, factories.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_172_TrOttm3] Cairo "had no specialized municipal administration or public institutions" yet such services were frequently provided for by waqfs institution (religious or charitable foundation).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_173_TrOttm3] The Wakala Dhulfiqar Katkhuda, built 1673 CE "was an enormous monument (2,625 square meters) ... 32 stores and 35 apartments around a courtyard."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_174_TrOttm3]
♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥ Rebuilt Egyptian irrigation systems.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_175_TrOttm3] Maintained Egyptian irrigation systems with wood brought from Anatolia. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_176_TrOttm3]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ present ♥ Aqueducts in Istanbul.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_177_TrOttm3] 111 drinking fountains built in Cairo during three centuries of Ottoman rule, 46 between 1626 and 1775 CE. Western area of Cairo two fountains built between 1517 and 1725 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_178_TrOttm3] Water was brought to fountains or to the door of residents manually, via camel and water carriers (up to 10,000 daily), and the fountains were built by waqf foundations and individuals rather than a municipal authority.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_179_TrOttm3]
♠ markets ♣ present ♥ Mehmet II built commercial centres including a covered bazaar in the Old City of Istanbul.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_180_TrOttm3] Endowment funds were invested in bazaars and shops. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_181_TrOttm3] Fixed economy in Istanbul. "The state required traders to guarantee the delivery wheat, salt, meat, oil, fish, honey, and wax directly to the palace and the capital city at fixed prices. Merchants were thus made agents of the state to meet the fiscal and provisioning needs of the capital." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_182_TrOttm3] In Cairo, the waqf instigated urban development of Ridwan Bey (a "powerful emir" in the late seventeenth century) over one hectare included a "commercial structure" containing shops.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_183_TrOttm3]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present ♥
♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ present ♥Selim Mosque (1522 CE). Sehzade külliye (1548 CE). Süleyman külliye (after 1550 CE). "The Sehzade and Süleyman külliyes were built by Sinan, the greatest Ottoman architect, whose masterpiece is the Selim Mosque at Edirne" (1569-1575 CE)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_184_TrOttm3]
♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ inferred present ♥Entertainers had a guild. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_185_TrOttm3]
♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ present ♥System of colleges within the empire trained teachers and muftis, law court judges.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_186_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Within Istanbul, there were schools outside the Palace itself. The first of these was the Galata Palace, a foundation of Bayezid II (1481-1512); the other, after 1536, was the Palace of Ibrahim Pasha on the Square of the Hippodrome. Nonetheless, the school in the Third Court remained the most prestigious."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_187_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Government archives.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_188_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Libraries. In the "traditional era" of the Ottoman Empire there only waqf libraries existed. These were founded in the 15th and 16th Centuries, and were supported by an endowment or charitable trust. "Almost 500 waqf libraries, spread throughout the vast Ottoman lands, were formed by Sultans, Sultans' mothers, government officials, scientists, and philanthropists who considered books and libraries essential. Majority were located in Istanbul, Bursa and Erdine (Adrianople). The two main varieties were madrassa libraries and specialist libraries. There were independent Ottoman waqf libraries, the first founded by the Koprulu family in 1678 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_189_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Public schools.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_190_TrOttm3] "The first Ottoman college was established in Iznik in 1331, when scholars were invited from Iran and Egypt to augment Muslim instruction in the new territories."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_191_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Tribute slaves for the Ottomon bureaucracy and military were educated in Palace schools. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_192_TrOttm3]
♠ Roads ♣ present ♥ Network expanded under Bayezid II (1481-1512 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_193_TrOttm3] In Cairo, according to Jean de Thevenot in 1658 CE "There is not one fine street in Cairo, but a great number of small ones that twist and turn, showing that the houses in Cairo were all built without benefit of a city plan."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_194_TrOttm3]
♠ Bridges ♣ present ♥ Bridge building. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_195_TrOttm3]
♠ Canals ♣ present ♥ In Egypt, Maqsud Pasha (1642-1643 CE) "ordered the dredging of two canals, the Khalij al-Hakimi and the Khalij al Nasiri, which were threatened with silting."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_196_TrOttm3]
♠ Ports ♣ present ♥ Istanbul. 1525 CE rebuilt naval base at Suez. 1530 CE added dockyard and warships at Basra. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_197_TrOttm3]
♠ height ♣ ♥ in m. The Sultan Ahmed Mosque or Blue Mosque, located in Istanbul, Turkey just 500 ft from the Hagria Sofia. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_198_TrOttm3] The mosque is the only one in the world to have six minarets, which contribute to its towering height of 70m. The height of the dome reaches 43m. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_199_TrOttm3]

♠ Script ♣ present ♥ In Egypt financial registers "were written in the special Siyaqat script, developed for financial purposes out of the Arabic script and introduced into the Treasury during the 10/16th century by the scribes who were sent to it from the Porte. The concise and regular nature of this script made it ideal for use in the limited space available in the registers, and is lack of the normal Arabic diacritical marks and violation of the usual rules for the formation and connection of Arabic letters made it incomprehensible to all but those especially initiated into the secrets of its formation and use." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_204_TrOttm3]

♠ Modern fortifications ♣ suspected unknown ♥"On the march discipline was fierce. Training had been equally strict, through games like the crit - javelin-throwing from horseback- and archery were also popular pastimes. Concern for smartness was such that it even hindered the adoption of untidy weapons like firearms. European observers were struck by the fugal diet of their enemies, the cleanliness of their camps, their well-ordered latrines and their lack of drunkenness, all of which accounted for the absence of disease among Ottoman armies. They were also amazed by the quietness of Turkish encampments."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_276_TrOttm3]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ present ♥ Present. Consisted of Janissaries, cavalry and artillery corps.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_277_TrOttm3]Janissaries were originally stationed in Istanbul. After the crisis of the mid-16th century they were moved to garrisons in the provinces.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_278_TrOttm3]
♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ absent ♥
♠ Total army ♣ [85,000-95,000]: 1550 CE; [100,000-200,000]: 1600 CE; [100,000-200,000]: 1650 CE ♥"Whether we accept the conservative figure of 100,000 or the more generous estimate of 200,000 as best representing the reservoir of potentially mobilizable timariots (together with their retainers), it remains a fact that in any given campaign only a proportion of these needed to be activated and deployed."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_279_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Figures for the early sixteenth century before the Ottoman conquest of Hungary in 1541 indicate a timariot army with a potential strength of approximately 90,000 men".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_280_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In 1527 CE 28,000 slave infantrymen, 70,000-80,000 cavalrymen (37,500 timar holders). Auxiliary garrison and frontier raiders financed by tax exemptions. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_281_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1400-1590 CE: timar holders and their retainers number about 50,000 and "formed the most important element in the Ottoman army."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_282_TrOttm3]
♠ Professional army ♣ [62,000-75,000]: 1600 CE; 70,296: 1670 CE ♥Table 3.5 in Murphey (1999) presents "Size and composition of sultan's standing army (kapu kulu) 1527-1670"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_283_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 18,689: 1527 CE; 29,175: 1574 CE; [62,000-70,000]*: 1597 CE; 75,868: 1609 CE; 70,296: 1670 CE *no data for artillery corps which was 2,124 in 1574 CE and then 7,966 in 1609 CE so expressed as range with Janissaries + Cavalry as baseline figure.

♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ [100-150] ♥ Istanbul-Baghdad 1800 km.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Figure depends on terrain, weather and road conditions and possibly also weight of baggage train and army mood (celebrations etc.). [MAJIDBENAM_REF_290_TrOttm3] In this case have used a theoretical figure based on average movement of about 15 km per day (including rest days), slightly higher average because the Balkans territory which gave an estimate of 13 km was particularly challenging.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_291_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 22 km per day[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"To avoid advancing too far ahead of its baggage trains, a normal day would see the army on the move for no more than about four to four-and-a-half hours. Proceeding at a relaxed walking pace for horses of three miles per hour and assuming no prolonged stops, this would allow the vanguard of the army to advance approximately 13.5 miles (22 km) in a day’s march. The rear guard and baggage trains proceeding at a slower pace (perhaps 2 mph) would need the better part of six hours to reach the advance camp."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_292_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Istanbul-Belgrade[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Murphey (1999) references a researcher who estimated time it would take to move the Ottoman army from Istanbul to Belgrade, a distance of about 1,000 km NB: measures closer to 800 km on Google maps, though it may depend on route taken. The estimate includes 20 days of non-movement for rest and river crosses. Over the days their was movement, the army marched at about 17.5km per day, with the average for the whole 77 days being 13 km per day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_293_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Istanbul-Baghdad[MAJIDBENAM_BR] A march to Baghdad in 1638-1639 CE "took 544 hours, spread over 197 days, of which 121 (61.5 per cent) were spent in march and 76 (38.5 per cent) at rest." However the return journey took 146 days which included 81 rest days, perhaps due to weight of loot or victory celebrations.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_294_TrOttm3]
♠ Sources of recruits ♣ foreign; domestic ♥"Both [yaya] and the müsellems were gradually relegated to second-line duties late in the 14th century, and by 1600 such units had either been abolished or reduced to non-military functions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_295_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR] yaya footsoldiers who were free Turkish farmers.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "horsemen .. known as müsellems (tax-free men)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_296_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Janissaries sourced from prisoners captured during raids.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Devsirme tribute from 1438 CE. In 16th Century about 1,000 boys taken per year from a province. The recruits were later divided into those who would serve the bureaucracy and those who would form the Janissaries. In 1582 recruits of non-devsirme origin, including free Muslims, were permitted to join the Janissaries. After 1648 CE, devsirme system not used to recruit for the Janissaries. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_297_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Recruited as tribute slaves from mostly Christian (Baltic-region) families. Baltic or Balkan?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"From 1582 non-devşirme recruits, the sons of Janissaries and free Muslims of many ethnic backgrounds were officially permitted to join."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_298_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Egyptian troops "were the principal source of the armies which conquered and occupied Yemen, southern Arabia, and Abyssinia and which fought the Frankish fleets for possession and control of the eastern seas, and its Emirs led the process of organization which followed these conquests."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_299_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Nomadic Bedouin formed auxiliary cavalry. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_300_TrOttm3]

♠ Torture ♣ present ♥"by the time of the battle of Mohacs in 1526, the akinji were well accustomed to being employed for penetrating enemy territory ahead of the main Ottoman Army. They would secue bridges and take prisoners for interrogation."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_316_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Ottoman authorities considered impalement primarily as a punishment for use as an example or warning for others. Death by impalement was most excruciating and when done correctly caused the victim to die slowly over a period of two or three days. ... Vlad Drakul first used such a grim and terrifying method of inflicting death. The Ottomans learned its use later. Impalement came into use, for example, during the 1453 siege of Constantinople as a means of terrifying the besieged defenders, and weakening their morale."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_317_TrOttm3]

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ inferred present ♥Evidence in the regions from Croatia: "The aim of this paper is to test the hypothesis of an increased level of interpersonal violence in Dugopolje during the late medieval period as testified by written sources. ... Most of the long bone injuries occurred as a result of accidents, probably due to rugged mountainous terrain, while a certain portion of trauma resulted from deliberate violence. ... Presented bioarchaeological data are in accordance with the written documents thus corroborating the claims of an increased level of deliberate interpersonal violence in the late medieval population from Dugopolje."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_324_TrOttm3]

List of Wars[edit]

Ahmet's Revolt (1512 CE-1513 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Ottoman-Mamluk (1516 CE-1517 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Celali Revolt (1519 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Mamluk Revolt (1523 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Siege of Rhodes (1522 CE) 88-89 The second problem to occupy Süleyman's attention was the island of Rhodes, a dangerous outpost of Christianity in an otherwise Ottoman sea. The pirates of Rhodes were capturing large numbers of ships bringing grain and gold from the new Arab provinces and carrying pilgrims to and from the Holy Cities, thus threatening the sultan's prestige as well as his pockets. Strategic as well as political reasons impelled Suleyman to neutralize Rhodes before moving into central Europe. A major expeditionary force mounted a prolonged siege during the summer of 1522. Pitted against the Ottomans were some 60,000 defenders manning one of the strongest forts known to the world at the time. Assault after assault was thrown back until Ottoman agents among the Jews and Muslim women enslaved by the Knights enabled the sultan's forces to break in and force a surrender (December 20, 1522). By its terms all inhabitants wishing to leave the island were allowed to go, and the Knights could take their weapons and other belongings. Those who remained were exempted from taxes for five years and given the same conditions of freedom of religion found elsewhere in the sultan's dominions. Thus in fact few inhabitants left. The Knights, however, were transported on papal ships to Malta, which they fortified and built into a new base of anti-Muslim operation in the heart of the central Mediterranean. With Rhodes in Ottoman hands, however, the eastern Mediterranean was now safe.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Mamluk Revolt (1527 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Siege of Belgrade (1520 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Ottoman-Hungarian (1526 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Celali Revolt (1526 CE-1527 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Siege of Vienna (1529 CE) With the Habsburgs preoccupied, Süleyman was able to reoccupy Buda (September 3, 1529) and the remainder of the country with little difficulty. He then mounted the first Ottoman siege of Vienna (September 27-October 15, 1529) in the hope of either capturing the Habsburg capital or at least so disrupting the Austrian military system that Ferdinand would be unable to threaten Hungary for some time to come. However, the season was too late. The attackers were unable to secure sufficient supplies and ammunition due to the early arrival of winter. Meanwhile, Charles was able to make peace with Francis at Cambrai (August 3, 1529) and to send reinforcements. While the suburbs of Vienna were largely destroyed, the walls pierced in many places and the defenders suffering severely, Vienna held out even after Ferdinand and most of his court had fled. The Janissary corps - already unhappy because the sultan had not allowed it to ravage Buda - again became restless. Turkish raiders began going through the Alps into southern Germany, reaching Ratisbon in Bavaria and Brunn (Brno) in Bohemia. Europe was in a panic. If the sultan had held out only a little longer, his forces might well have broken into Vienna, where they could have remained for the winter before pushing onward. But the combination of difficulties finally convinced Süleyman to return to Istanbul for the winter. Therefore, the siege was broken off and Vienna saved for Christianity.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Ottoman-Ethiopian (1529 CE-1544 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Little War in Hungary (1532 CE-1566 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First CE-1681 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Tarnovo Uprising (1688 CE-1689 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).
♠ RA ♣ Rosalind Purcell; Enrico Cioni ♥ Purcell responsible for most research and coding; Cioni responsible for additional research and coding, and general editing of section.
♠ Expert ♣ ♥ The name of the historical or archaeological expert who supervised coding and checked/improved/approved the result. If more than one expert was involved, list all.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Description of the Ritual System

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ sur ♥ "For the presenting of compliments and renewal of bonds of obedience by the sultan’s servitors, there existed other opportunities - on fixed occasions, timed to coincide with the annual celebration of the two religious festivals of the lesser and greater feasts ('Id) or with the commencement of a new reign - but all of these ceremonies represented much scaled down, one-day versions of the celebration of sultanic might and majesty. On these cyclically repeating occasions, attendance was necessarily sporadic and partial when compared to the attendance of the sultan’s officials at the multi-week surs, where participation was not just strongly encouraged but virtually obligatory and universal." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_325_TrOttm3] Surs were grand festivals that celebrated the circumcision of the sultan's son(s). "During the entire course of the minimum two-week celebrations - occasionally stretched to six weeks or longer, to approach the legendary figure of 40 days and 40 nights of public rejoicing on exceptional occasions - there was a general relaxing of the usual restrictions on public behaviour and a toleration for drinking, carousing and other forms of social intercourse that were frowned upon or actively prohibited during ‘normal’ times. The interaction between, and the mutual enjoyment shared by, members of the major-faith communities of the Muslims, Christians and Jews were maximized during these secular festivals, called shenlik (‘rejoicings’), in a way that was never possible on the other occasions for communal celebration, which by definition were bound to the specific religious calendars of each particular faith. In general terms, we know that the Muslim ulema took a rather disapproving stance regarding such celebrations, but others viewed them as a necessary and salutary form of release from the everyday strain of unrelenting devotion to work and duty. The elaborate celebrations for the circumcision feast of the sultan’s male offspring provided such respite only once, or at most twice, during a reign, but the communal celebrations and city illuminations (donanma) commonly ordered on the occasion of notable military victories could and did recur more frequently. Such universal celebrations served to break down the barriers not just between communities of the faithful but also between the refined and the unrefined social classes (the havas and the avam) who, especially during the less formally structured evening entertainments, mingled more or less indiscriminately on the streets of Istanbul and took part in what the disapproving would have described as orgiastic or bacchanalian delights offered to onlookers and spectators from all walks of life. Whether one approved or disapproved, there can be no doubt that, during their brief duration, the festivals served as platforms and stages for both social leveling and social inclusion." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_326_TrOttm3] Entertainment and food were provided for the masses, while higher officials enjoyed more orderly banquets. An important component of the ritual were lengthy processions of dignitaries and officials, as well as representatives of the artesanal classes, who demonstrated their loyalty to the sultan through the presentation of gifts and demonstrations of submission. This event was the prime vehicle through which the sultan demonstrated his power, influence, and generosity, strengthening the bond between himself and his people; and, at the same time, it was a way for him to smooth his children's eventual inheritance of the throne, as their coming of age would forever be associated, in the minds of people, with lavish celebrations and general rejoicing [MAJIDBENAM_REF_327_TrOttm3].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ once per generation ♥ "The elaborate celebrations for the circumcision feast of the sultan’s male offspring provided such respite only once, or at most twice, during a reign[...]." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_328_TrOttm3]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once per generation ♥ "The elaborate celebrations for the circumcision feast of the sultan’s male offspring provided such respite only once, or at most twice, during a reign[...]." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_329_TrOttm3]

♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ "As event organizers and hosts, the sultan and the grand vizier, who acted as a kind of grand marshal and master of ceremonies rolled into one, managed to preserve order and decorum in the processions and feasts organized for the ulema and ümera, each taking part in the assigned order of hierarchical precedence and on the individual days set aside for their attendance and entertainment, but also successfully prescribing the terms under which the general mob of city dwellers and urban underclass (fukera) took part in the events." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_333_TrOttm3]

♠ fasting ♣ absent ♥ "Food distributions and other charitable gestures on a grand scale, associated with and staged at recurring intervals throughout the sur celebrations, were the element of the celebrations that made the deepest and longest lasting impression on the collective memory and the sultan-host spared no efforts to ensure that his reputation as pater familias to the meanest and neediest of his subjects remained intact. The banquets offered to select companies of invited guests, practically on every day of the festival, were exceptional for the refinement and variety of the dishes offered for the guests’ enjoyment, but for sheer munificence and liberality the general feasts were not just impressive but truly memorable." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_336_TrOttm3]
♠ vigil/sleep deprivation ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by the sources [MAJIDBENAM_REF_337_TrOttm3].
♠ fear ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by the sources [MAJIDBENAM_REF_338_TrOttm3].
♠ humiliation ♣ absent ♥ "Those with whose past service and present submission of homage and gifts the sultan was satisfied received confirmation in office and donned their hilats, but such attendance also always provided the ruler with the opportunity to express his dissatisfaction by withholding recognition to those officials who had lost his favour. On-the-spot dismissals and demotions did not make up a prominent feature of the sur proper, which was supposed to be an occasion for general rejoicing, but sometimes the dismissals were only just barely deferred until the completion of the festival which thus made the connection with the office holder’s most recent personal encounter with the sultan or patron inescapably obvious." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_339_TrOttm3]
♠ disgust ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by the sources [MAJIDBENAM_REF_340_TrOttm3].
♠ poisoning/dysphoria-causing drugs ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by the sources [MAJIDBENAM_REF_341_TrOttm3].
♠ pain ♣ present ♥ The circumcision of the sultan's sons was usually the event nominally celebrated by the sur, and, alongside the sultan's sons, "at repeated intervals during the sur the sultan also earned both religious merit and popular credit by offering to meet the costs associated with the circumcision procedures for several thousand young men from around the empire whose birthdays coincided with that of the prince or who were close to entering puberty at the time of the sur celebrations. This honour was reserved in particular for children from underprivileged families and orphans, which meant that, for those participants, the sur celebrations were not just generically but also personally memorable." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_342_TrOttm3]

[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Cohesion
♠ honour code ♣ present ♥ "In content, form and spirit the surs, especially those held in the proto-imperial era, closely resembled the annual gathering of the clans in pastoral society, designed to reconfirm the bonds of loyalty to the chiefs of confederated tribes." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_353_TrOttm3] Most significantly, "the shared memories and associations formed during the few weeks of common celebration during the sur ceremonies made up a permanent part of the collective identity of the future sovereign’s subjects, and it was such associations rather than the stripped down, businesslike and all too brief and sometimes rather formal and arid ceremonies for confirming a sultan’s position of power on his accession to the throne that carried the greater and more lasting significance and emotive weight. The process of personalizing the future sultan’s rule through dynamic and interactive associations built up by the princes during their minority eased the transition to independent rule by providing the eventual successor with a personality and reality in public imagination and perception well in advance of his succession." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_354_TrOttm3]

[MAJIDBENAM_REF_355_TrOttm3]

[MAJIDBENAM_REF_359_TrOttm3]
♠ Orthopraxy checks ♣ present ♥ "To the untrained eye, there appeared to be an erratic and uncontrolled alternation between daytime order and night-time chaos which allowed participants at random intervals to disregard and overturn the normal rules for polite public behaviour with impunity. But this seeming carnival atmosphere gives a misleading impression of the actual forceful influence of the sultan’s commanding presence. It was only at his signal and with his permission that the interludes of riotous behaviour both commenced and ceased." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_360_TrOttm3]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Friday prayer ♥ "The Friday prayer is a duty of the community; it contains a short sermon, khutba*, which consists of two parts. It gives some advice usually based on the Koran or the hadith, and a prayer for the ruler of the government. To be mentioned in the khutba meant to be the legitimate ruler. Women rarely attend the Friday prayer but prefer to pray at home.The daily prayers are explained in the Koran as acts of humility and adoration. At the end of the prayer the Muslim can mention his/her personal requests. While traveling or in war or under duress, one can perform the noon and afternoon or sunset and night prayers together. One also has numerous opportunities to perform additional prayers of two or more rak'a each, e.g., during solar or lunar eclipses; when performing the rain prayer; when entering or leaving the house or the mosque—in short, some pious Muslims use every opportunity to perform a brief salat. The belief in the purifying power of ritual prayer is intense; the Prophet compared it to a stream of water that washes off sins five times a day. The performance of the prayer at the prescribed time constitutes ideally a means of educating Muslims to punctuality, cleanliness, and, since there is no ranking in the mosque, equal participation in the life of the community. Prayer can also lead to ecstatic experiences, and when one observes a praying Muslim who is oblivious of everything around him and seems to have drawn himself, as it were, out of this world to stand humbly before the Lord, one realizes best whence Islam derives its vital strength.To perform the salat, a clean spot suffices, be it in the fields, in the train, or in one's shop or office. A small prayer rug guarantees the cleanliness of the place. However, even in the Prophet's lifetime a place for congregational worship was established, called masjid, "the place where one prostrates" (hence the word Mosque). The great mosque, where the Friday prayer is held and which was found in each quarter, is called jami'*, "the gathering [mosque]."Mainly in the eastern part of the Islamic world, one finds small open prayer places called musalla*, and for the gathering of large congregations at the two 'id*, the end of Ramadan and the Feast of Offerings, one sometimes uses an 'idgah*, a vast enclosure with a prayer niche."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_369_TrOttm3]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_370_TrOttm3]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_371_TrOttm3]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_372_TrOttm3]
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. Noon prayer on Fridays. Expert input needed for exact duration. at this time wiki scraper not able to read decimal, so cannot code less than 1 hour e.g. 0.05 for 15 minutes.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priesthood. Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ All Muslims, though women would frequently pray in private instead.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_373_TrOttm3]

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Friday prayer ♥ "The Friday prayer is a duty of the community; it contains a short sermon, khutba*, which consists of two parts. It gives some advice usually based on the Koran or the hadith, and a prayer for the ruler of the government. To be mentioned in the khutba meant to be the legitimate ruler. Women rarely attend the Friday prayer but prefer to pray at home.The daily prayers are explained in the Koran as acts of humility and adoration. At the end of the prayer the Muslim can mention his/her personal requests. While traveling or in war or under duress, one can perform the noon and afternoon or sunset and night prayers together. One also has numerous opportunities to perform additional prayers of two or more rak'a each, e.g., during solar or lunar eclipses; when performing the rain prayer; when entering or leaving the house or the mosque—in short, some pious Muslims use every opportunity to perform a brief salat. The belief in the purifying power of ritual prayer is intense; the Prophet compared it to a stream of water that washes off sins five times a day. The performance of the prayer at the prescribed time constitutes ideally a means of educating Muslims to punctuality, cleanliness, and, since there is no ranking in the mosque, equal participation in the life of the community. Prayer can also lead to ecstatic experiences, and when one observes a praying Muslim who is oblivious of everything around him and seems to have drawn himself, as it were, out of this world to stand humbly before the Lord, one realizes best whence Islam derives its vital strength.To perform the salat, a clean spot suffices, be it in the fields, in the train, or in one's shop or office. A small prayer rug guarantees the cleanliness of the place. However, even in the Prophet's lifetime a place for congregational worship was established, called masjid, "the place where one prostrates" (hence the word Mosque). The great mosque, where the Friday prayer is held and which was found in each quarter, is called jami'*, "the gathering [mosque]."Mainly in the eastern part of the Islamic world, one finds small open prayer places called musalla*, and for the gathering of large congregations at the two 'id*, the end of Ramadan and the Feast of Offerings, one sometimes uses an 'idgah*, a vast enclosure with a prayer niche."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_406_TrOttm3]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_407_TrOttm3]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_408_TrOttm3]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_409_TrOttm3]
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. Noon prayer on Fridays. Expert input needed for exact duration. at this time wiki scraper not able to read decimal, so cannot code less than 1 hour e.g. 0.05 for 15 minutes.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priesthood. Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ All Muslims, though women would frequently pray in private instead.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_410_TrOttm3]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Id al Fitr ♥ 'Id al-Fitr is the best loved Islamic festival, and it was already an important part of the Islamic calendar by the seventh century CE [MAJIDBENAM_REF_443_TrOttm3]. Descriptions of the festival are surprisingly rare, especially as regards how it was celebrated in the past, but given that most Islamic practices demonstrate a great degree of continuity with the past, so descriptions of how the festival is celebrated in modern times may apply to how it was celebrated in the past as well. 'Id al-Fitr is celebrated at the end of the month-long Islamic fast known as Ramadan. "As in the Friday afternoon congregational prayer of the weekly Day of Assembly, the Eid morning prayers at the mosque should be preceded by a bath and attended with clean (often new) clothes. After breakfast, men, women, and children gather at an outdoor prayer meeting, the musallah, in front of a white wall adorned with a niche, the mihraab pointing in the direction of Mecca (like the one in every mosque). Women are given the option of praying at home--as they mostly do the rest of the year. The prayers consist of two units (rakat) plus the six-to-sixteen additional takbir recitations of Allahu Akbar ('God is Most Great'). These set them apart from Friday prayers, as does the fact that they are followed instead of preceded by a two-part sermon (khutba). [...] This enthusiasm erupts spontaneously on the aptly named Eid-al-Fitr, the festival of the breaking of the month-long Ramadan fast. Still, by then, passions have been so well trained that few unbridled excesses are in evidence at the happy family celebrations of warm community that follow or at the fairground attractions that are set up in some places [...]. Indeed, restored social harmony is on display everywhere over up to three days, with people greeting and congratulating each other in the streets as well as on afternoon visits to relatives, friends, and sick people. Many gifts are offered, particularly to the children of less fortunate families. At home, the family celebrates around a noontime festive meal [...]. Dead relatives are also remembered as their graves are visited that day." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_444_TrOttm3] There is also a specific type of charity given on the occasion of 'Id al-Fitr, known as Zakat-ul-Fitr [MAJIDBENAM_REF_445_TrOttm3].

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Arafat wuquf ♥ The key ritual within the wider sequence of rituals that makes up the Hajj. “On the morning of the ninth, [pilgrims] proceed towards ‘Arafat, a plain 14.5km (nine miles) from Mina, where the central rite of the Hajj, wuquf (standing) on ‘Arafat, takes place between noon and sunset, during which time many heartfelt prayers are offered. The scene reminds Muslims of the gathering on the Day of Judgement. If any pilgrim misses this event, his/her Hajj is not valid and has to be done again another year. This does not apply to any other rite, all of which can be done over a longer period or compensated for with an offering. This is the time for the pilgrims to read the Qur’an, glorify God and pray most earnestly for forgiveness and everything else they wish for. Particularly recommended for glorification and repetition is this prayer, ‘There is no God but Allah alone, with no partner. Dominion and praise belong to Him, who gives life and death. Goodness is in His hands and He has power over everything.’ This just gives a succinct summary of the Islamic faith and connects the individual pilgrim to what Ibrahim and Muhammad brought and uttered. Pilgrims normally face towards the Ka‘ba while reciting these prayers, as they do for the five daily prayers, believing that, by doing this, their prayers are more likely to be accepted.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_480_TrOttm3]. This ritual is coded here as dysphoric due to the fact that pilgrims are required to stand for several hours (between noon and sunset) under the sun’s heat. A contemporary account, dating to 1997, notes that the “heat is virtually unbearable” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_481_TrOttm3]. However, it is worth noting that, due to peculiarities of the Islamic calendar, this ritual may sometimes be performed at a time of the year when the temperature is less hot than at other times, and when there are fewer others between noon and sunset than at other times. Moreover, a few informal online sources suggest that those who cannot stand in the sun for the duration of the ritual are allowed to be in the shade and/or sit down. Still, though relatively mild, this appears to be the most dysphoric ritual within Sunni Islam.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ The main acts of the Hajj take place in the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar [MAJIDBENAM_REF_482_TrOttm3].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every Muslim should undertake the Hajj pilgrimage at least once in their lifetime.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_483_TrOttm3]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Duration ♣ 6 ♥ hours, on average. The ritual takes place between noon and sunset.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ all Muslims[MAJIDBENAM_REF_484_TrOttm3]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥Dysphoric elements
♠ fasting ♣ inferred present ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_485_TrOttm3], but, because participants must devote all their energies in prayer, it seems likely that they were not allowed to eat. Then again, this may not count as fasting due to the relative brevity of the ritual (between 5 and 7 hours, depending on the time of the year when it is performed on any given year).

♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ absent ♥ [Text from sacrifice codings by Agathe] No source could be found that explicitly states Islam’s condemnation of human sacrifice. However, the fact that no source could be found that even ‘’mentions’’ human sacrifice in connection with Islam (beyond the occasional lurid, and dubious, tabloid headline), combined with the fact that a lot of information is available on the beliefs and practices underlining this religion, strongly suggests that human sacrifice is not part of the Islamic ritual repertoire. ““Our” present religions, Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, sometimes called Abrahamic religions, have the theme of human sacrifice right at their inception, with the founding father, Abraham. We are accustomed to the story, which remains a text of “terror and trembling,” as Kierkegaard put it. Abraham is commanded by god to “sacrifice” his only son Isaac, and he is willing to do so (Gen. 22). The Hebrew term for this sacrifice is colah, “make to rise upwards,” as fire and smoke rise to heaven;10 the action is to cut the throat with a knife and to burn the corpse on wood—the knife and wood expressly mentioned in the text. The human victim must be fettered in order to receive the deadly cut. Abraham does obey his god’s command, without even an attempt at protest. The text enjoys the paradox: “the son that you love”; “and the two walked together” (Gen. 22.1-19: 2;. In Muslim tradition, the place where Abraham “bound” his son is the rock on the temple hill of Jerusalem, which, since Khalif Omar, is covered by the “temple dome” with its golden cupola. Of course, an angel stops Abraham’s action at the last moment, pointing to an animal for sacrifice. The text says God was “testing” (nissah) Abraham, and his submissive obedience is highly rewarded. The text also states that god accepted the ram “instead of” (tachath) Isaac. One way to discard the terror of the story is to introduce a perspective of historical progress: God was replacing human sacrifice by animal sacrifice. This could be termed an advance in civilization. The Islamic interpretation comes close to this interpretation, as it makes this a founding myth for el eid.”
[MAJIDBENAM_BR][MAJIDBENAM_REF_513_TrOttm3][MAJIDBENAM_BR]

♠ Impeachment ♣ absent ♥ It was a dynasty state and the ruler passed on his power to a male heir.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_516_TrOttm3] "There never, it seems, was a formal mechanism for policy making. All decisions in theory were the sultan’s own. What mattered, therefore, was the character of the sultan, and the individuals or factions who had his ear."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_517_TrOttm3] The Ottoman Sultan had absolute authority like an emperor and could not be impeached through an official process.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ ♥

The prince who first reached the capital, took hold of the treasury, and got the support of the army had the best chance to become the new sultan, while the fate of his brothers and their sons was execution."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_518_TrOttm3] The 'race to Istanbul' contest was ended from Mehmed III. "Mehmed III was the last heir to the throne to serve as a provincial governor, and thus to acquire experience of public affairs ... Henceforward, for fear of revolts, all princes of the blood were confined permanently to the Seraglio, cut off from the world in a building to be known as the Cage, and thus benefiting from no such experience."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_519_TrOttm3] "When Ahmed died in 1617, he was survived by both his brother and several sons ... A court faction secured the enthronement of Mustafa I instead of Osman, thus further solidifying the end of royal fractricide and giving way to a new principle of seniority in succession practices."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_520_TrOttm3]

♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 4 ♥ Note: Succession to the throne was a 'race to Istanbul' transition contest, with the cost of losing usually execution: "Proximity to Istanbul and the throne was crucial in the Ottoman Empire, where no particular rule governed the succession of sultans. The prince who first reached the capital, took hold of the treasury, and got the support of the army had the best chance to become the new sultan, while the fate of his brothers and their sons was execution."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_535_TrOttm3] The 'race to Istanbul' contest was ended from Mehmed III. "Mehmed III was the last heir to the throne to serve as a provincial governor, and thus to acquire experience of public affairs ... Henceforward, for fear of revolts, all princes of the blood were confined permanently to the Seraglio, cut off from the world in a building to be known as the Cage, and thus benefiting from no such experience."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_536_TrOttm3] *1520* No transition contest The narratives I have so far read do not mention any 'race to Istanbul' contest for the accession of Suleiman I but we need an expert to confirm that's the case. *1566* No transition contest Selim II. Rivals already dead. The bribe of the Janissaries was perfunctory as there is no obvious rival: "However, in 1566, when Suleyman died in Hungary, Selim's succession was not without complication. To protect the enthronement and accession process, the brilliant grand vizier, Sokollu Mehmed Pasha, kept Suleyman's death a secret until Selim reached the army in Belgrade. Even then, Selim's accession was not immediately sanctioned by the Janissaries. When the army returned to Istanbul, the Janissaries did not allow Selim to enter the palace until he paid the culus gratuity, a gift of money customarily paid by sultans to the military upon accession to the throne."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_537_TrOttm3] *1574* Murad III's accession was a 'race to Istanbul' contest: "On the night of his arrival in Istanbul, seasick after a long crossing from his seat of government at Magnesia, Murad had ordered his five brothers to be strangled."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_538_TrOttm3] *1595* Mehmed III's accession was a 'race to Istanbul' contest: "The first action of his son, who succeeded him as Mehmet III, was to have his nineteen brothers strangled by mutes - the largest fratricidal sacrifice in Ottoman history. ... Meanwhile six pregnant slaves, their favourites from the harem, had been sewn up in sacks and thrown into the Bosporus, lest they give birth to claimants to the throne."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_539_TrOttm3] *1603 No transition contest* Ahmed I: "When Mehmed III died later in the same year, a faction in the inner palace decided that Ahmed should succeed his father as sultan before any vizier or government leader had been consulted. This was an unusual succession ... Ahmed did not opt for this long-established practice of royal fratricide ..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_540_TrOttm3] *1617* Mustafa I: "When Ahmed died in 1617, he was survived by both his brother and several sons ... A court faction secured the enthronement of Mustafa I instead of Osman, thus further solidifying the end of royal fractricide and giving way to a new principle of seniority in succession practices."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_541_TrOttm3] "The death of Ahmed I thus led to an unprecedented situation in Ottoman history: several princes were eligible for the throne, all of whom resided within the Topkapi Palace. ... Mustafa I was soon deposed by the faction that opposed his succession".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_542_TrOttm3] *1618* No transition contest Osman II. *1622* Mustafa I (second reign): "clerics petitioned Mustafa's mother to agree to the deposition of her son in favor of 11-year-old Prince Murad, the oldest surviving son of Ahmed I. The Queen Mother concurred, only pleading that her son's life be spared."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_543_TrOttm3] *1623* No transition contest Regency of Murad IV: "Because he was too young (at age 12) to rule, Murad's mother assumed the role of de facto regent, controlling the business of rule by her clique in the government."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_544_TrOttm3] On Murad IV's accession the state treasury was virtually empty and the viziers were approaching foreign ambassadors for loans but it seems that support of the Janissaries was still essential. "Murad, however, contrived to find 3,040 purses from his own private treasury, which was distributed to the Janissaries within a month of his accession - regardless of the fact that they had previously agreed to forgo it."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_545_TrOttm3] *1632* Formal accession of Murad IV: "For events, in the all-too-familiar form of a mutiny of the household troops, precipitated Murad's assumption of effective power as Sultan. In 1632 the sipahis of the Porte massed together in the Hippodrome for three successive days while the shops closed their doors and terror reigned, both in the city and in the Palace itself." The revolt was initially against "no fewer than seventeen specified officials and favourites" rather than the sultan himself but even after they were given the Grand Vizier to kill the rebels and the deposition of the Mufti, they "continued to publicly discuss that of Murad himself." Murad IV's bloody crackdown stopped the revolt.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_546_TrOttm3] *1640* No contested transition Ibrahim I: "there was no rival prince who could claim the Ottoman throne."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_547_TrOttm3] *1648* No contested transition Regency and reign of Mehmed IV: "In 1648, at the age of seven, he was placed on the throne following a coup that deposed his father, who was assassinated soon after."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_548_TrOttm3] Janissaries revolted in 1651 "against the payment of their salaries in debased coins ... conspiring to dethrone Mehmed and replace him with his brother Suleyman."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_549_TrOttm3] Three years after start of reign does not count as a transition contest unless the payment in debased coins they were unhappy about happened in the first year.
